  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_958fd688-c450-4f8e-b673-fcc01b68c0a4'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer4151827458736051533">Orphaned at an early age, Philip Ashley is raised by his benevolent older cousin, Ambrose. Resolutely single, Ambrose delights in Philip as his heir, a man who will love his grand home as much as he does himself. But the cosy world the two construct is shattered when Ambrose sets off on a trip to Florence. There he falls in love and marries - and there he dies suddenly. Je</span>
  <span id="freeText4151827458736051533" style="display:none">Orphaned at an early age, Philip Ashley is raised by his benevolent older cousin, Ambrose. Resolutely single, Ambrose delights in Philip as his heir, a man who will love his grand home as much as he does himself. But the cosy world the two construct is shattered when Ambrose sets off on a trip to Florence. There he falls in love and marries - and there he dies suddenly. Jealous of his marriage, racked by suspicion at the hints in Ambrose's letters, and grief-stricken by his death, Philip prepares to meet his cousin's widow with hatred in his heart. Despite himself, Philip is drawn to this beautiful, sophisticated, mysterious Rachel like a moth to the flame. And yet... might she have had a hand in Ambrose's death?</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">You're not supposed to know - the idea is that you make up your own mind. I think the important thing is to remember through whose eyes we're viewing <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">You're not supposed to know - the idea is that you make up your own mind. I think the important thing is to remember through whose eyes we're viewing Rachel, and what his mental state might be.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/9864326-katie"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1512342787i/9864326._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/9864326-katie">Katie</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    No. He was not hanged. He identifies with the man he saw hanged when he was 7 because the convict was stuck in a purgatory between Heaven and Hell. Ph<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    No. He was not hanged. He identifies with the man he saw hanged when he was 7 because the convict was stuck in a purgatory between Heaven and Hell. Phillip was emotionally in the same place because he killed Rachel (by contriving to walk across the bridge he knew was broken) and because he does not know if she killed Ambrose and attempted to kill him or not. It implies he will live out his life in the constant state of torment of wondering. <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });
      $('span_class_gr-hyperlink_sort_order_span_tip_884').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>    </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14162685112390679129"><b>WOW! I liked MY COUSIN RACHEL even better than REBECCA!</b><p>This <b>dark</b> old-fashioned style mystery gives the reader plenty of clues to contemplate, some suspicious evil characters to dissect and others who's naïvety and impulsive acts will make you want to scream with frustration.....or it did me.</p><p>As for the ending....I loved it, but you'll have to decide for yourself if Rachel is innocent or guilty of a crime(s)......but IMHO <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ she is absolutely, positively 100% guilty, guilty, guilty!!!</span></p></span>
  <span id="freeText14162685112390679129" style="display:none"><b>WOW! I liked MY COUSIN RACHEL even better than REBECCA!</b><p>This <b>dark</b> old-fashioned style mystery gives the reader plenty of clues to contemplate, some suspicious evil characters to dissect and others who's naïvety and impulsive acts will make you want to scream with frustration.....or it did me.</p><p>As for the ending....I loved it, but you'll have to decide for yourself if Rachel is innocent or guilty of a crime(s)......but IMHO <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ she is absolutely, positively 100% guilty, guilty, guilty!!! <b>And</b> I <i>still</i> cannot believe <i>all</i> the clues Philip destroyed, but then.....<i>thankfully,</i> it did not matter in the least. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></p><p>What a great classic tale!</p><p>
  <i>(recommend reading with no prior knowledge going in)</i>
</p><p>
  <b>Update: March 7, 2017</b>
</p><p>Lucked out <i>(Yes!)</i> and caught the 1954 movie version with Richard Burton and Olivia de Havilland on TMC just 5 minutes in.....</p><p>Really enjoyed it, but definitely not as much as the book. The written word was more mysterious and had a stronger undercurrent of evil.....Still a good flick though.</p></span>

<div id="review_261988067" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/261988067" />
    <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters"><img alt="Elyse  Walters" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514246949p2/597461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/261988067?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elyse  Walters" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters">Elyse  Walters</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781579125691" href="/book/show/50239.My_Cousin_Rachel">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer261988067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15399455616055794422">I started reading Daphne du Maurier just this year.....a 2017 *du Maurier* newbie! <br/>The first book I read was "The Scapegoat", then "Rebecca", then "The Flight of the Falcon".....and this morning I finished "My Cousin Rachel". <br/><br/>"My Cousin Rachel", is Daphne du Maurier's 3rd highest rated book. <br/>"Rebecca" is rated as the #1 popular favorite-- followed by "Jamaica Inn" as the #2 most popular. <br/><br/>I won't be surprised with a new movie of "My Cousin Rachel", soon, if this book takes the #2 popular spot soo</span>
  <span id="freeText15399455616055794422" style="display:none">I started reading Daphne du Maurier just this year.....a 2017 *du Maurier* newbie! <br />The first book I read was "The Scapegoat", then "Rebecca", then "The Flight of the Falcon".....and this morning I finished "My Cousin Rachel". <br /><br />"My Cousin Rachel", is Daphne du Maurier's 3rd highest rated book. <br />"Rebecca" is rated as the #1 popular favorite-- followed by "Jamaica Inn" as the #2 most popular. <br /><br />I won't be surprised with a new movie of "My Cousin Rachel", soon, if this book takes the #2 popular spot soon. <br /><br />Regardless 'which' Daphne du Maurier book one chooses to read... the reader has NO QUESTION ABOUT THE AUTHORS TALENTS. SHE CAN TELL A STORY LIKE NOBODIES BUSINESS!!! <br /><br />Since there are 17,462 Ratings on Goodreads - 1, 638 reviews- with an overall rating of 3.9 on Goodreads ..... rather than write another traditional review-- <br />I'm going to write a little letter to three characters in this story and one dog. <br /><br /> Dear Ambrose, <br /> I'm sorry your life was cut short. I'm happy you experienced romantic-soulful love....but again, I'm sorry it was cut short. I'm sorry you suffered just before you died - sounded like you were in excruciating pain physically- and mentally. <br />What I'm most sorry about though - disappointed - is the way in which you raised Philip. I don't think you did him any service. He idolized you - was dependent on you emotionally- spiritually - mentally - and even physically in ways. You feed him 'your' opinions and judgments about women.... which was basically they weren't needed in your lives. Given the size of your estate - and the many servants you hired - it would have made sense if some of your employees were men and some women. But.... you made sure that all servants in the house, including the Gardner we're all men. <br />How dare you project your views so rigidly upon this innocent boy. You molded him his life -- not just sheltered it - but actually spoon feed him your beliefs. Shame on you for doing that. <br />After you died.... Philip didn't have a healthy sense of 'self'. He was left alone in the world --- a younger clone of 'you'. But that didn't even work.... did it?? As Philip begins to discover he might not have known you as well as he thought. <br />Well, Ambrose.... what is done is done. Philip had to figure things out on his own --- which isn't SO BAD..... it's something we all must do, but in the best of all world's - you might have done things a little different by him while he was in your care. <br />Bottom line: Philip had some problems - you must have foreseen these showing up sooner or later...... <br />AND..... Philip loved you. You loved him.<br />Rest In Peace Ambrose!!<br /><br /> Dear Philip, <br /> Oh, how I worried for you. At times I adored you - other times I wanted to scream at you: "WAKE UP"!!!! Your life was so small you couldn't even see how much it had stifled you. I did respect your authentic struggle between feeling jealous of Ambrose and Rachel's happiness in their new marriage--- and your guilt for having such feelings. You were being real and honest with yourself - facing 'what's so'. You weren't blaming anyone - or lashing out negatively against anyone just because of your own negative feelings. <br />Philip you became mysterious to me once you turned 25 years old-- as if your birthday was THE LAUNCH you had been constructing -preparing for - your entire life. I wasn't sure who you were any longer. I not sure you knew either. It looked like you were acting out impulsively to me -- in ways that perhaps Rachel might do.....<br />but I don't think so, Philip. I actually don't think either - you or Rachel ever acted impulsively. I think you were both playing a master chess game. <br />I am sorry you lost Ambrose. I'm sorry you lost him the way you did. I was sorry that you hurt. I was very sorry for your loss of Don ( your doggie). I found it somewhat interesting that right after Don died the first thought that ran through your mind was noticing that Rachel was caring for you - for the first time perhaps - more than Ambrose. You seemed to deny your 'own' feelings for your loss as Don, saying to Rachel, ....."Don did not suffer". I was thinking ...."like hell". If you had something fall on 'your' head..... could you really say.... "oh I didn't feel a thing"? <br />You were so in chanted by Rachel at that moment, Philip, your dog who had been with you your entire life took a second seat. What a fool you were! <br />I don't think you really meant to do any harm.... yet I thought you were a insensitive..... like a child.....you were a little narcissistic about yourself. And why? You were woman- starved your entire life! I was hoping that he would just go f#%k your brains out! You needed time to catch up with 'growing yourself up'.<br />You certainly didn't need to even consider jumping into a marriage at age 25 as a virgin man! It was your own fault if Rachel had you tied by her little finger. <br /><br />NOTE..... for friends who have NOT read this book.... I HAVE GIVEN NO SPOILERS AWAY!!!! None!<br /><br /> Dear Rachel, <br /> Oh my dear woman..... aren't you the enchantress--mischievous aren't you? Bossy-endearing?......"Philip, Go to Bed"! How funny! I did like that line you said a 'few' times. I was wondering why you didn't just say, "come to my bed".... but I knew why really....( he would have)....you were not 'that' foolish! You had better strategic moves, didn't you? <br />Oh..... and how you familiarize yourself with the estate and stories after stories about Philip from Ambrose. Plotting were you? You could seem so delicate. The entire staff adored you....you were quite brilliant at being you. You had me believing in your thriftlessness--your unpolishedness. But you were actually quite skilled and sophisticated ....... You had a natural masterly authoritative way about yourself. Tears included. I'm not sure I fault you though --- Philip and you 'both' played a dangerous game with the hearts of love - money - and -control. <br /><br /> Dear Don,<br /> I'm sorry you died so suddenly. You were 15 years old. I loved when you snuggled near the fireplace with Philip..... Rest in peace, Don! You were a treasure in this story. <br /><br /><br />The REAL WINNER .......goes to Daphne du Maurier....she knows how to develop characters - how to spin a story - how to involve the readers ...... A+ <br /><br />Oops... we don't grade these books - do we?/! - we rate them! 5 stars!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15399455616055794422" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer5895120410303126645">du Maurier's ability to write an settling story, coupled with her haunting prose is one of my favorite things. I could see how some might call this story boring, it's very slow - BUT, to me the slow moving mystery, disturbing characters, and hints that made me flip back and forth between who I suspected... I loved it so much. Then you have du Mourier's ability to write an ending that both doesn't say enough, and says just enough to make me SURE I know what happened. <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ she was total</span></span>
  <span id="freeText5895120410303126645" style="display:none">du Maurier's ability to write an settling story, coupled with her haunting prose is one of my favorite things. I could see how some might call this story boring, it's very slow - BUT, to me the slow moving mystery, disturbing characters, and hints that made me flip back and forth between who I suspected... I loved it so much. Then you have du Mourier's ability to write an ending that both doesn't say enough, and says just enough to make me SURE I know what happened. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ she was totally coning him. And her friend was in on it. She probably did it to tons of guys  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> but the next person could think something totally different and have really good reasons to think so! That's the thing! du Maurier's endings give enough info that you KNOW what happened, but you never actually know. And it's so freaking brilliant </span>

<div id="review_2068003157" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2068003157" />
    <a title="Julie " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5210022-julie"><img alt="Julie " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356906542p2/5210022.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2068003157?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie " href="/user/show/5210022-julie">Julie </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=classic-mystery">classic-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=e-book">e-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=axis360">axis360</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=historical-mystery">historical-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2068003157" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6996895265572490050">My Cousin Rachel by Daphne du Maurier is a 1952 publication. This version is the 2013 Little, Brown and Company publication. <br/><br/>A scathingly wicked masterpiece-<br/><br/>This book has a very heavy atmospheric quality that drew me in right from the start. In fact, I was so transfixed, I put aside everything else and gave myself over to its allure. <br/><br/>Phillip Ashley was raised by his cousin, Ambrose, a confirmed bachelor. They are content with their arrangement, and with Phillip’s role as heir to Ambrose’s legacy</span>
  <span id="freeText6996895265572490050" style="display:none">My Cousin Rachel by Daphne du Maurier is a 1952 publication. This version is the 2013 Little, Brown and Company publication. <br /><br />A scathingly wicked masterpiece-<br /><br />This book has a very heavy atmospheric quality that drew me in right from the start. In fact, I was so transfixed, I put aside everything else and gave myself over to its allure. <br /><br />Phillip Ashley was raised by his cousin, Ambrose, a confirmed bachelor. They are content with their arrangement, and with Phillip’s role as heir to Ambrose’s legacy. <br /><br />However, Phillip’s complacency is upended quite unexpectedly when Ambrose travels to Florence, meets ‘our cousin, Rachel’, a woman with a connection to their family, and immediately falls under her spell. <br /><br /><br /> In a shockingly brief period of time, Ambrose goes from wedded bliss to death, leaving Phillip furious with Rachel, suspecting her of contributing to his cousin’s demise in some way, shape, or form. <br /><br />But, when Rachel shows up at his doorstep with Ambrose’s belongings, Phillip too finds himself enchanted by Rachel and soon forgets all about his earlier jealousies and suspicions. Until…<br /><br />I have several books in my collection written by this author, but the only one I’ve read is, ‘Rebecca’,<br /><br />When this book began to recirculate due to a motion picture and a ‘great library reads’ promo, my interest was highly piqued. <br /><br />Finally, I managed to work it into my schedule, but I wish I had discovered this gem sooner! <br /><br />This book has so many layers, yet it progresses slowly, adding sinister nuances, bit by bit, until I found myself helplessly trapped in the author’s, oh so clever web before I knew what had happened, maybe a bit like Phillip, eh?<br /><br />This book combines Gothic mystery, strong feminist undertones, psychological mind games, vanity, guilt, and suspicion, which nearly made me delirious with delight. But, it also left me with a plethora of theories and speculations. <br /><br />Is Rachel guilty… or was it really Phillip? Or was she Innocent? Both? Was Phillip just a pawn in Rachel’s master plan? Did Phillip needlessly destroy his life? <br /><br /><br />While I would ordinarily feel extreme frustration with an author for this open- ended conclusion, in this case, it’s exactly what makes this book a classic. Written back in 1952, the book was a little ahead of its time, although some may have only taken it at face value. Nevertheless, here we are in 2017, still talking about it, still pondering on its mysteries, analyzing, and theorizing about it, which is a testament to the author’s talent and genius. <br /><br />This book was practically tailor made for my reading tastes, so naturally, it gets five big stars!! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6996895265572490050" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2014090890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16180654568002642176">I loved this story! It reminded me so much of Rebecca, both have that gothic atmosphere, the bit of romance, the slow moving story that builds such tension, and the dimwitted main character. I thought briefly this might even surpass Rebecca as my favorite, but how can you top 'Last night I dreamed I went to Manderley again'? Though not too shabby here with 'They used to hang men at Four Tunings in the old days'.<br/><br/>My Cousin Rachel tells the story of obsession. Philip is a young gent, who is grievin</span>
  <span id="freeText16180654568002642176" style="display:none">I loved this story! It reminded me so much of Rebecca, both have that gothic atmosphere, the bit of romance, the slow moving story that builds such tension, and the dimwitted main character. I thought briefly this might even surpass Rebecca as my favorite, but how can you top 'Last night I dreamed I went to Manderley again'? Though not too shabby here with 'They used to hang men at Four Tunings in the old days'.<br /><br />My Cousin Rachel tells the story of obsession. Philip is a young gent, who is grieving over the loss of his cousin, who also raised him, Ambrose. Ambrose was recently married to his cousin Rachel and became quite ill and died. Now Rachel goes to Cornwall to return the belonging of Ambrose to Philip, and the two finally meet. Oh there is jealousy, deceit, and such an obsession in this wonderful tale. I don't want to tell anymore as to give anything away. Just read this one and decide for yourself about cousin Rachel. Is she evil or not?<br /><br />I listened to this one via audio and the narrator was awesome! Jonathan Pryce, the actor, adds so much to this story, building the tension even more as he reads this tale. If you are a fan of audios, I highly suggest this one. I loved every minute of this and the tension and suspense went right to the end. A top read and top audio for the year, and a new book to add to my favorites and list of those I would enjoy a re-read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16180654568002642176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2072894627" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2072894627" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65909643-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515603070p2/65909643.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2072894627?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/65909643-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65909643-michael?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2072894627" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12959883004038190518">This was a buddy-read with James (Jay), author of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36140949.Watching_Glass_Shatter' title='Watching Glass Shatter by James J. Cudney' rel='nofollow noopener'>Watching Glass Shatter</a>, and I'm so happy we read this. I'd rate it 4.5* just to distinguish it from Rebecca, which gets the full five.<br/><br/>The book actually began a little slowly for me, and it felt almost discombobulated, as if DuMaurier were shuffling toward the storyline. But then Rachel appears and everything clicks masterfully into gear. DuMaurier expertly conjures a brooding, ambiguous atmosphere, and from the very beginning we wonder about this</span>
  <span id="freeText12959883004038190518" style="display:none">This was a buddy-read with James (Jay), author of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36140949.Watching_Glass_Shatter" title="Watching Glass Shatter by James J. Cudney" rel="nofollow noopener">Watching Glass Shatter</a>, and I'm so happy we read this. I'd rate it 4.5* just to distinguish it from Rebecca, which gets the full five.<br /><br />The book actually began a little slowly for me, and it felt almost discombobulated, as if DuMaurier were shuffling toward the storyline. But then Rachel appears and everything clicks masterfully into gear. DuMaurier expertly conjures a brooding, ambiguous atmosphere, and from the very beginning we wonder about this bewitching woman who seizes our narrator, young Philip, in the grip of her Italianate charm. The fact that she may have been responsible for Philip's guardian's death is something he dismisses with the enthusiasm of the young, until....<br /><br />Plot summaries abound, and I won't add to them here. The language of the book is utterly beguiling, those languid fluid sentences sweep us through the plot and make us take notice of all sorts of subtleties: the cut of a dress, the gleam of an eye, the inflection of a voice, and of course the natural world in all its infinite variety: <b>"The wind of the day before had blown itself up-country, taking the rain with it, and at noon the sun had broken through and the sky was clear. There was a salty brightness in the air, lending a zest to walking, and you could hear the running swell of the sea as it broke upon the rocks fringing the bay."</b><br /><br />Once I got past the first few pages, I felt myself in the hands of an expert storyteller. The plot moved through its gears with precision, and even when I knew or sensed what was coming, it was a sublime pleasure to see DuMaurier spin it out, taking her time, building suspense so perfectly. <br /><br />Oddly enough, though, it was the very expert nature of the narrative that makes this book, for me, slightly less interesting than Rebecca. Rebecca has always seemed to me almost Sui generis, and it enchanted me equally yet surprised me more; it seemed more complex and difficult to pin down, a narrative of almost infinite slipperiness and ambiguity. My Cousin Rachel, by contrast, is less layered and more straightforward, even though it has its own twists and turns and beautiful ambiguities. <br /><br />Still, this is a book that will reside with me for some time and one that I'll no doubt return to, if only to see and admire how DuMaurier handles a certain aspect of plotting or character, how she masterfully drops her clues into the narrative stream, how she keeps the pages turning, knowing exactly when to slow down and admire a landscape, and when to speed us through. This is a thoroughly pleasurable book, and one that I highly recommend.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12959883004038190518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3184834804" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3184834804" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3184834804?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/29759152-my-cousin-rachel">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Robin by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=english">english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3184834804" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15981921704606988223">You don't have to be a lover of the gothic to love <i>My Cousin Rachel</i>. First and foremost, this is scintillating, seductive storytelling by the author who brought us <i>Rebecca</i>. <br/><br/>I must say, while the plot of <i>Rebecca</i> is more complex, boasting an ending that twists and surprises, I think I liked this more. The simplicity of <i>My Cousin Rachel</i> makes it a pleasure to read. Such sly intimations, such lurking dread! <br/><br/>Quick synopsis: Philip's cousin Ambrose, a long time woman-hater, goes traveling and surprise</span>
  <span id="freeText15981921704606988223" style="display:none">You don't have to be a lover of the gothic to love <i>My Cousin Rachel</i>. First and foremost, this is scintillating, seductive storytelling by the author who brought us <i>Rebecca</i>. <br /><br />I must say, while the plot of <i>Rebecca</i> is more complex, boasting an ending that twists and surprises, I think I liked this more. The simplicity of <i>My Cousin Rachel</i> makes it a pleasure to read. Such sly intimations, such lurking dread! <br /><br />Quick synopsis: Philip's cousin Ambrose, a long time woman-hater, goes traveling and surprises everyone by sending news that he's met and married a woman (guess who). Then he surprises everyone by... dying. Under questionable circumstances. Soon after, Rachel turns up on Philip's doorstep. Did she or didn't she? She might have - but she's so pretty! The mystery is confounding.<br /><br />It's naughty too, with erotic elements that bring to mind to the sadly under-read gothic treat <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2584972486" rel="nofollow noopener">Sweetheart, Sweetheart</a>. Don't even <i>try</i> to tell me your heart isn't palpitating when dear Philip, in a great manly gesture, bestows to Rachel his 'family jewels' on the bed. Oh, this is old fashioned jewel-bestowing at its finest!<br /><br />You'll turn those pages, gobble them up, because du Maurier is just that good. The ambiguity she creates is <i>Turn of the Screw</i> good. You'll change your mind about Rachel a dozen times before and after you've reached the story's end. <br /><br />With all this mental ping-pong, you might not even notice you're reading a gothic novel, although it has all the classic tropes too - the old and venerated estate, (unreliable?) letters received after the sender has died, beautiful gardens, a long and mysterious seduction, the death of an animal.<br /><br />It seems to me Ms. du Maurier is underrated as a writer. Her prose is elegant. Her story is addictive, and so well constructed, too. She's smart and stylish, both. Plus, don't forget, gothic as hell.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15981921704606988223" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_674953685" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/674953685" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/674953685?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/11300065-my-cousin-rachel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-m-p">read-authors-m-p</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=mystery-crime">mystery-crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer674953685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10878905524340043112"><b>My Cousin Rachel </b> was published in 1951, and is a gripping story of suspense. It very much recalls the feel and style of her earlier mystery novel "Rebecca" of 1938.<br/><br/>The novel's narrator is Philip Ashley, a young man who had been orphaned at the early age of seven. He was raised by his bachelor uncle Ambrose, the owner of a large country estate on the Cornish coast. In the first chapter, we learn Philip's life story up to the age of 24. <br/><br/>Ambrose had been a very unconventional guardian, teaching Ph</span>
  <span id="freeText10878905524340043112" style="display:none"><b>My Cousin Rachel </b> was published in 1951, and is a gripping story of suspense. It very much recalls the feel and style of her earlier mystery novel "Rebecca" of 1938.<br /><br />The novel's narrator is Philip Ashley, a young man who had been orphaned at the early age of seven. He was raised by his bachelor uncle Ambrose, the owner of a large country estate on the Cornish coast. In the first chapter, we learn Philip's life story up to the age of 24. <br /><br />Ambrose had been a very unconventional guardian, teaching Philip his alphabet by using the initials of swear-words, for instance, but Philip clearly idolised him. As Philip grew up, they ran the estate together in an all-male household, where Ambrose taught Philip to have nothing to do with women; that they were a <i>"race apart." </i> Although Philip feels himself to be mature, the reader infers from the start that for much of the narrator's life he has been overprotected, and his experience very restricted. The only women in his life have been slight acquaintances, whom he usually found laughable. He does have one female friend, Louise Kendall, the daughter of his godfather, but he views her as young and immature.<br /><br />During this chapter, in which Philip shares his thoughts, it is clear that something has gone badly wrong. There are frequent mysterious allusions. Philip is clearly tormented by feelings of guilt towards someone he calls "Rachel" - possibly the Rachel of the novel's title. <br /><br /><i> "No one will ever guess the burden of blame I carry on my shoulders;" he says, "nor will they know that every day, haunted still by doubt, I ask myself a question which I cannot answer. Was Rachel innocent or guilty? Maybe I shall learn that too, in purgatory." </i><br /><br />The reader is intrigued to know whatever could have happened. The narrator seems bent on mentally torturing himself with reminiscences. <br /><br /><i>"Some instinct should have warned her that to stay with me would bring destruction, not only to the phantom that she encountered, but finally, in the end, upon her too." </i> <br /><br />Having trapped us in her web of mystery and intrigue in the very first chapter, Daphne du Maurier proceeds to use her mastery and control of the narrative to entice us further in.<br /><br />She does this by slowing the novel right down. Although Philip is speaking in flashback, his memories of the events he is recounting from chapter 2 onwards feel very much like the present. The language is at times poetic; the images lush and beautiful, especially the parts set in Italy. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Ambrose although only 43 had had to spend the winters in a warmer climate because of his health. His letters told Philip that while he was in Florence, he had fallen in love and married a cousin of theirs called Rachel, the widow of an Italian nobleman, Sangalletti. Sangalletti had been killed in a duel, leaving her childless and in a precarious financial position. They wanted to travel to England, but it would not be for a long time as there were many financial affairs to sort out. <br /><br />Philip was devastated by this news, but also ashamed that he could not be pleased for Ambrose, while everyone else seemed delighted for the happy couple. Ambrose's letters became increasingly sporadic, and unlike the carefree Ambrose of old. When he did write he complained of the relentless sun, the stuffy atmosphere of the villa Sangalletti and terrible headaches. Ambrose also said that a friend and advisor of Rachel's called Rainaldi had recommended that he see a different doctor, whom he referred to as <i>"a cut-throat. They are liars, the whole bunch." </i> He said he could trust no one. <i> "She watches me all the time." </i> This resolved Philip upon immediately dashing across to Italy to find out what was happening.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The reader too is firmly gripped in the author's clutches by now. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ We know Ambrose's father had a brain tumour; we know Philip is an impressionable naive young man; we know his jealousy of Ambrose's unknown new wife. He constantly shifted between viewing her as as some kind of overbearing bossy dragon, or as a prissy feminine creature making unreasonable demands. We have many possible scenarios in our minds, but nothing substantial.<br /><br />On Philip's arrival in Florence the tension is cranked up another notch when he discovers that Ambrose has died there in suspicious circumstances, and that Rachel has left. The description of the countryside, the villa Sangalletti and the servants through Philip's eyes make it clear that he cannot understand how Ambrose could have ever been happy here. Already we have the dichotomy. Is Philip's view of the world impossibly sheltered, or has there been some sort of conspiracy? Philip is badly shocked by the realisation that he will never see Ambrose again. Is this colouring his views? He calls on Rainaldi, who seems startled when he sees Philip, but quickly recovers himself and explains that Ambrose's condition had deteriorated quickly, with his behaviour becoming very strange and that this was because of pressure on his brain. The reader knows this to be possible, as Philip's godfather, Nick Kendall, has explained that Ambrose's father had himself died of a brain tumour, having suffered terrible headaches beforehand. Nevertheless, Philip feels intense dislike and mistrust for Rainaldi and hatred of the unknown Rachel <br /><br /><i> "the shadowy hated figure of that woman I had never seen… she had become a monster, larger than life itself." </i> <br /><br />Philip returns to Cornwall, to try to cope with the devastation of Ambrose's death. Shortly afterwards his godfather receives a letter from Rainaldi. The death certificate confirmed that Ambrose's cause of death was a brain tumour. In addition, Ambrose had never changed his will in Rachel's favour so Philip is still heir to the estate. He will inherit the estate and all the consequent duties in a few months, when he becomes 25. Again we have the jarring note. Why did Ambrose not change his will? Why is there no provision whatsoever for his widow?<br /><br />During the next two weeks Philip builds up a mental image of the hated Rachel whom he blames for wrecking both their lives. But then word comes to say that Rachel has arrived by boat at Plymouth. Apparently she has all Ambrose's possessions with her and wants to return them to Philip. It is impressed on Philip by his godfather that he must, however reluctantly, invite her to stay with him briefly. The servant Seecombe pulls out all the stops to prepare the house for a female visitor, unearthing silver which Philip had never seen before and so on. On the day Rachel is due to arrive, Philip goes out so that there will be no one to receive her. He determines to do as little as possible for his visitor despite the servants' excitement. When Philip does eventually meet his cousin he is brusque to the point of rudeness, and tries hard to maintain a cold demeanour.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />This part of the novel is lighthearted, providing some relief from all the previous tension. The reader sees Philip's dilemma. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Rachel is a quiet self-contained character, not at all what he expected her to be. She has a mischievous sense of humour, which deflects all Philip's bombast. Her unpretentiousness and knowledge of the estate - the lands, buildings and all the tenants - unnerve Philip, and he begins to learn that she and Ambrose were very close. Philip finds himself at ease with her; all thoughts of anger, hatred and fear disappear very quickly. <br /><br />The next day Philip shows Rachel around the estate and introduces her to the tenants in the surrounding farms. She is instantly welcomed and liked by all, seeming to have a true empathy with people. Philip is startled by this, especially when Seecombe refers to her as <i>"the mistress" </i> and it is made clear both how much she is liked for herself, and also how everybody would have loved Ambrose to have brought his new wife to live on the estate. <br /><br />It only takes a couple of days for Rachel to have completely destroyed any intentions Philip had to make her feel unwelcome. On the contrary he now resents the fact that she was supposed to stay with the Kendalls, and seems to want to keep her all to himself. The reader deduces that this impressionable young man is becoming besotted with this mysterious, older woman. His friend Louise tells him that she is beautiful, but he claims on the contrary that she is <br /><br /><i> "the most ordinary woman I have ever met." </i><br /><br />And in contrast during the narrative he feels himself <i> "blundering,…tactless,…unfeeling, oafish and illbred." </i><br /><br />Rachel herself is portrayed very engagingly; we do not doubt that she loved Ambrose and is grieving for him. Rachel has brought many plants from Florence and she spends a lot of time working in the gardens with Tamlyn, the head gardener. Evidently Rachel is an expert on plants and herbs and often provides advice on the use of herbal remedies to the staff in the house and the families in the nearby farms. <br /><br /><i>"We are very old, and very wise, who come from Florence," </i> <br /><br />she says. Rachel becomes well-loved by all, and Philip comments that she seems to know far more about many of the people than he has ever managed to learn in his entire life. <br /><br />Louise, however sees her differently. <br /><br /><i>"How simple it must be," </i> she says at one point,<i> "for a woman of the world like Mrs Ashley, to twist a young man like yourself around her finger." </i> <br /><br />Her point of view may be consistent throughout the novel, but is it the correct one? She is, after all, possibly in love with Philip and therefore jealous. Rainaldi too is depicted as unreliable; he is <i>"disliked intensely" </i> by the narrator. But is Rainaldi's judgement sound? We have glimpses of his own view of Rachel, <br /><br /><i>"the nature of her impulses has not always led her into happiness." </i><br /><br />And what of Nick Kendall's view that  "There are some women… good women very possibly, who through no fault of their own impel disaster."  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The reader feels continually on edge reading this novel. Daphne du Maurier is in total control, manipulating our loyalties. In addition, one of the unusual and almost hypnotic elements of this novel is the changing moods created by the author. The main character's feelings are constantly mirrored by the manner of describing the locations, and especially the weather. When Philip is dreading the arrival of his visitor he goes walking in the wind and rain. He gets wet <i> "nearly to the skin."</i> Again after a violent disagreement we are again told that it is a cold, rainy day. The wind is blowing and by the time he returns home he is chilled and wet.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[When he and Rachel are searching through Ambrose's boxes of belongings,  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br /><i>"The rain was lashing at the windows of my old room, and a patch of damp had appeared on the ceiling. The fire, which had not been lit since last winter, burnt with a false crackle." </i> <br /><br />And in the middle of this heightened tension, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Philip discovers another note from Ambrose <i> <br /><br />"it's a disease of course… like kleptomania or some other malady, and has no doubt been handed down to her from her spendthrift father… I dare not let her have command over my purse or I shall be ruined." </i><br /><br />During another section, when Philip is in a sort of limbo, not understanding his own feelings for Rachel or whether they are truly reciprocated,  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Nature seems held in suspended animation; in contemplative mood. <br /><br /><i>"My nights of vigil held a quality harder to bear. There was a sort of beauty to them, cold and clear, that caught at the heart and made me start in wonder. From my window the long lawns dipped to the meadows, and the meadows to the sea, and all of them were white with frost, and white too under the moon. The trees that fringed the lawns were black and still… suddenly from the hush and stillness I heard that high sharp bark of a vixen… away in the distance in the open park… and now the full moon toppled the trees and held the sky and nothing stirred on the lawns beneath my window… here about me was a wealth of beauty that we might have shared." </i> <br /><br />Although this passage is much shortened, the stillness is almost tangible; the reader almost feels able to reach out and touch it.<br /><br />Later, when Philip feels that he has succeeded with an audacious plan to secure the future he desires most, he <br /><br /><i> "rode home with a reckless feeling in my heart… I would have sung aloud but I could not keep to a single tune. The hedgerows were green, and the willows were in bud, and all the honeyed mass of golden gorse in bloom. It was a day for folly and high fever." </i><br /><br />And when Philip is again happy with anticipation, <br /><br /><i> "It was a night for walking. I did not run, but for all that I achieved the beacon hill. The moon, so nearly full, hovered, with swollen cheek, above the bay, and wore about his face the look of a wizard man who shared my secret… there were the flickering lights of the little towns along the western coast, and our own harbour lights to the east as well… I climbed down… and laughing to myself at this folly most sublime plunged into the water." </i><br /><br />Contrast this with an episode when he feels himself betrayed and cannot see a way to proceed, <br /><br /><i> "The bluster that should have been in February and March had come at last. Gone was the mellow warmth of the past weeks, the smooth sea, and the sun. Great clouds with dragging tails, black-edged and filled with rain, came scudding from the west, and now and again with sudden bursting fury emptied themselves as hail. The sea was a turmoil in the western bay." <br /><br /></i> And later, <i> "The clouds came lower than they had before, turning the mist, cloaking the trees on the opposite shore. </i><br /><br />Threaded through the entire novel is the tension set up between the two main characters. Daphne du Maurier is a master at ambiguity as well as suspense. She holds her audience in thrall throughout, making the reader constantly sway between one theory and another, believing in one character and doubting another, only to have this turned on its head by a chance occurrence.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ A letter from Ambrose, found in an old overcoat by Sam Bate, the tenant of East Lodge, states, <br /><br /><i> "One thought possesses me, leaving me no peace. Are they trying to poison me?" </i> <br /><br />This is discovered in the middle of the story, and is the longest letter found so far. And a little later, <i> "She has done for me at last, Rachel my torment." </i> But who is being honest, who deceitful? Has the pressure in Ambrose's brain made his hallucinations yet worse? Is it true as he says that<br /><br /><i> "Money, God forgive me for saying so, is, at the present time, the only way to her heart." </i>? <br /><br />Louise says of Rachel, near the end, <br /><br /><i> "She came to England, and to Cornwall, for a purpose, which she has now accomplished."</i>  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Is anyone duplicitous - or not? Is anyone impossibly naive - or anyone overly suspicious? The author keeps the doubts and suspicion going right until the end of the novel. <br /><br />So does the reader ever discover the answers? Well now, that would be telling! One sentence maybe sums it up <br /><br /><i> "Only cold hard facts, twisted to distortion." </i> <br /><br />But which ones?<br /><br /><br /><b>Edit:</b> WARNING - Only click to expose this spoiler if you have already read the novel:<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The ambiguous ending here is a favourite device of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2001717.Daphne_du_Maurier" title="Daphne du Maurier" rel="nofollow noopener">Daphne du Maurier</a>. In an interview from 1977, she was asked, <br /><br /><i>"In My Cousin Rachel, was Rachel good or evil?"</i> and she replied, <br /><br /><i>"Lots of people have asked that and, to tell the truth, I don’t know. You see, <u>I was Philip when I wrote the book</u> ... When one is writing a novel in the first person, one must be that person."</i><br /><br />Interesting, isn't it? And you have it there from the horse's mouth. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><u>Here are links to my reviews of:</u><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/674959231" rel="nofollow noopener">Jamaica Inn</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/651439997" rel="nofollow noopener">Rebecca</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/739005968" rel="nofollow noopener">The House on the Strand</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/651436308" rel="nofollow noopener">The Scapegoat</a><br /><br /><u>and one play:</u><br /> <br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/950386078" rel="nofollow noopener">The Years Between</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10878905524340043112" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2759800917" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2759800917" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464987365p2/16254355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2759800917?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781478985051" href="/book/show/27108770-my-cousin-rachel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2759800917" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16985761192503941305">I have been struggling with this review a bit and I think I know why. If you have Rebecca as a point of reference before you read this one, I think you may struggle to review this one, too. If I did not have Rebecca, I would probably go with 4 to 4.5 stars for sure. With Rebecca, it is still great, but 3.5 to 4 instead. I am rounding up to 4 because it is still a very good book, just not Rebecca good!<br/><br/>While intense and mysterious, I found that the intensity and mystery plateaued early on in the b</span>
  <span id="freeText16985761192503941305" style="display:none">I have been struggling with this review a bit and I think I know why. If you have Rebecca as a point of reference before you read this one, I think you may struggle to review this one, too. If I did not have Rebecca, I would probably go with 4 to 4.5 stars for sure. With Rebecca, it is still great, but 3.5 to 4 instead. I am rounding up to 4 because it is still a very good book, just not Rebecca good!<br /><br />While intense and mysterious, I found that the intensity and mystery plateaued early on in the book and kind of stayed there throughout. Even getting into the final scenes in the book, everything just unfolded at the same level. Now, this is just how it was for me. Others may find the resolution more intriguing and high intensity than I did.<br /><br />Another thing about the plot and the mystery for me was that I don’t really feel it developed all that much. After the first third of the book I got the idea of what we as readers should be wondering about and that never really changed or grew; du Maurier just kept piling on more details about what was already going on and what had happened, and I was over here waiting for intricate and mind-blowing twists or side journeys. But, that didn’t really happen.<br /><br />So far it seems I have focused on the negative . . . or at least what didn’t work for me. It is only right that I make sure to note that despite what I already mentioned, I did find the story to be interesting and I like du Maurier’s writing very much. As an overall experience, I am not sorry in the least that I gave it a try. Again, I think it mainly comes down to trying to follow up Rebecca being a very big challenge!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16985761192503941305" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2055676839" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2055676839" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2055676839?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/29759152-my-cousin-rachel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=perfect-novels">perfect-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2055676839" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5357363641433393983">What's better than this book? What author on earth is better than Daphne du Maurier? What have we been doing, all this time? Reading impenetrable bollocks like <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/609469890' title='My review' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ulysses?</a> This book has a scene where Philip throws a rope up to Rachel in her bedroom window and she, who might have murdered his family, hauls the basket on the rope up hand over hand, and then he climbs up after and watches while she spills all of his family jewels out of the basket onto her bed. And then they fuck on them. I realize th</span>
  <span id="freeText5357363641433393983" style="display:none">What's better than this book? What author on earth is better than Daphne du Maurier? What have we been doing, all this time? Reading impenetrable bollocks like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/609469890" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses?</a> This book has a scene where Philip throws a rope up to Rachel in her bedroom window and she, who might have murdered his family, hauls the basket on the rope up hand over hand, and then he climbs up after and watches while she spills all of his family jewels out of the basket onto her bed. And then they fuck on them. I realize that in real life this would be super uncomfortable for whoever's on the bottom getting gems in their shoulderblades, but in a book it's a scene of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1314594412" title="My review of Far from the Madding Crowd" rel="nofollow noopener">Tom Hardyan vividness.</a> It's pure gothic magic.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1580954163i/28910508._SX540_.png" alt="du-maurier" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Daphne du Maurier, deciding whether to poison you</i><br><br>And why do we talk about gothics as though they're second-class? Second to what? <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1020784583" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Mrs. Dalloway?</a> Why? Most gothics are about relationships too, after all, from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/177295526" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Lady Audley's Secret</a> on down: families, and whether we know ours, and how trust can warp. They'll throw some poison or a ghost in to liven things up - and why shouldn't they? Wouldn't Mrs. Dalloway be a little better with poison or a ghost? But their interests are in the home - home, as it grows around you like kudzu. <blockquote>
<br>I wondered how it could be that two people who had loved could yet have such a misconception of each other and, with a common grief, grow far apart.</blockquote><br>And what theme is more grand than that? <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1103112149" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Gravity's Rainbow?</a> That book has its head so far up its ass that it's come out its own dick, which is coincidentally its grand theme. <br><br>One of the things du Maurier is, here and in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/402881581" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Rebecca,</a> is a master of ambiguity. The basic plot here is that Philip's guardian, Ambrose, goes on vacation; meets a dark woman; falls in love and dies under suspicious circumstances. The woman arrives at what's now Philip's home and sets her eyes in turn on him. <b>Or:</b> Ambrose, and then Philip, rotted with malice and distrust, set out to destroy a powerless woman. Du Maurier is so good at laying hints in each direction - you change your mind from chapter to chapter, and she never makes you feel cheated. Each step is fair. She's even borrowed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/951467852" title="My review of Moonstone" rel="nofollow noopener">Wilkie Collins's old letter trick,</a> embellishing and undermining the main story with a trickle of letters that fill in the backstory and which may not themselves be trustworthy. <blockquote>
<br>“There are some women, Philip,” [Ambrose] observed, “good women very possibly, who through no fault of their own impel disaster. Whatever they touch, somehow turns to tragedy."</blockquote><br>Du Maurier deals with the subconscious a lot: she's brilliant at communicating to you things her characters don't even know themselves. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/751154485" title="My review of Remains of the Day" rel="nofollow noopener">Ishiguro</a> is the only author who can match her for telling stories under stories. She has all the tricks: unreliable narrators, mysteries, epistolaries. She's the entire arsenal.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1580954163i/28910507._SX540_.jpg" alt="cousin-rachel" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Rachel Weisz and who cares in My Cousin Rachel (2017)</i><br><br>We <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/07/06/books/daphne-du-maurier-enthusiast.html?_r=0" title="Great NY Times piece about du Maurier" rel="nofollow noopener">call du Maurier's prose purple,</a> but I reject that as a valid description of anything. I deny the term altogether. There are a lot of ways to write a sentence - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/231237802" title="My review of Turn of the Screw" rel="nofollow noopener">Henry Jamesian</a> circuitry, DFWianly <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/382588682" title="My review of Infinite Jest" rel="nofollow noopener">footnoted,</a> ornately carved <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1962767185" title="My review of As I Lay Dying" rel="nofollow noopener">Faulkner.</a> Du Maurier's sentences are exciting, and they always do exactly what she wants them to. I have no interest in dividing sentences up into castes.<br><br>But if we're to divide authors into castes - and we are, that's what we do when we talk about classics - then why isn't du Maurier at the top? This is a flawless novel. It's perfect in every way. Every word is in service to the story; it's a real story about real things; it's always a page-turner. It's not a guilty pleasure, to be read when you're tired of serious literature. It <i>is</i> the serious literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5357363641433393983" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2301199602" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2301199602" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7365405-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522094190p2/7365405.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2301199602?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/7365405-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=9-read-2018-03-march">9-read-2018-03-march</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=1-fiction">1-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2301199602" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2609624943120653137"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Why This Book</u>
  </i>
</b><br/>After reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17899948.Rebecca' title='Rebecca by Daphne du Maurier' rel='nofollow noopener'>Rebecca</a> several years ago, I placed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18869970.My_Cousin_Rachel' title='My Cousin Rachel by Daphne du Maurier' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Cousin Rachel</a>, another of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2001717.Daphne_du_Maurier' title='Daphne du Maurier' rel='nofollow noopener'>Daphne du Maurier</a>'s famed novels, on my To Be Read (TBR) shelf. Earlier this year, a Goodreads buddy, Michael, and I were chatting about various books when we decided to do a buddy read together, selecting this wonderful Gothic edition. We were both interested to see if it lived up to the hype and how it compared to the author's other words. We agreed on early March and got to it this week. I'</span>
  <span id="freeText2609624943120653137" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Why This Book</u>
  </i>
</b><br />After reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17899948.Rebecca" title="Rebecca by Daphne du Maurier" rel="nofollow noopener">Rebecca</a> several years ago, I placed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18869970.My_Cousin_Rachel" title="My Cousin Rachel by Daphne du Maurier" rel="nofollow noopener">My Cousin Rachel</a>, another of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2001717.Daphne_du_Maurier" title="Daphne du Maurier" rel="nofollow noopener">Daphne du Maurier</a>'s famed novels, on my To Be Read (TBR) shelf. Earlier this year, a Goodreads buddy, Michael, and I were chatting about various books when we decided to do a buddy read together, selecting this wonderful Gothic edition. We were both interested to see if it lived up to the hype and how it compared to the author's other words. We agreed on early March and got to it this week. I've only started doing buddy reads in the last few months, but they are quite fun... I recommend them.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <u>Approach &amp; Style</u>
  </i>
</b><br />I purchased the Kindle Reader version from Amazon to read on my iPad. It contains ~350 pages and took me four days to read. The novel is written in first person point of view and told from the perspective of Philip Ashley, a 24-year-old English man set in a somewhat unknown time, but likely the early/mid twentieth century given some of the details in the background setting. The language is intense and full of amazing imagery and astounding descriptions.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <u>Plot, Characters &amp; Setting</u>
  </i>
</b><br />The novel centers around the Ashley family. Philip's parents die when he is less than a year old, but his cousin Ambrose raises him in their England home. At some point years later, Ambrose unexpectedly marries a widow named Rachel who is half-Italian and grew up in Tuscany. It's an odd pairing, as she has a bit of a reputation for husband-hunting and spending lots of money. After ~2 years, Ambrose mysteriously dies and Rachel disappears. Philip is distraught, but searches for her in Italy. Rachel eventually shows up in England looking to meet her pseudo-stepson, and that's when the story really begins to get interesting. There's an air of darkness concerning Ambrose's death--was Rachel involved? She has a suitor of sorts who follows her from Tuscany--yet both claim there is nothing but friendship. Philip intends to crucify his cousin Rachel after reading a few letters from his late cousin, Ambrose; however, things take a surprising turn when more secrets are revealed and there's a bit of romance developing in the background. Add in a few traditional English families, an inheritance upon Philip's 25th birthday, and a possible proposal to/from a neighboring family... and you've got quite a Gothic story unleashing it's power on you.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <u>Key Thoughts</u>
  </i>
</b><br />1. du Maurier truly engages the reader with lyrical and ethereal descriptions of everything going on in the story. You will feel like you are sitting at a table in the house watching everything occur around you. The super-fine details are what challenge your intellect to decide what is real and what is not.<br /><br />2. As a plot, it's classic -- did she or didn't she kill him? But here's the interesting part... that question hardly ever comes up in the book. It's not a mystery in terms of researching the past to see if murder actually happened. It's entirely psychological in the relationship between Philip and Rachel... where you listen to the words or what isn't said, think about whether you trust either of them... and in the end, you just wish you could have spoken to Ambrose yourself to get the answer.<br /><br />3. I went back and forth multiple times deciding whether I liked Rachel and Philip as characters and as human beings. Humanity and kindness are huge themes in this novel. Attitude and disinterest are also keen to make themselves present within the relationships. Sometimes I wanted to throttle both, other times, the tenderness was admirable. The last few chapters truly push the envelope in terms of engaging more doubt before there is a final reveal.<br /><br />4. While reading the first ~75 pages, I was also editing my novel. I had on my 'writer glasses' and couldn't stop analyzing the word choice in du Maurier's initial chapters. It was disconnected and hard to attach myself, too. I also found a few words that were repeated a couple of times on the same page (a pet peeve for me in my own writing) and after the third or fourth, I slapped myself and realized it wasn't important. 99 amazing words on every page and 1 every so often that didn't work. That's way too high of a percentage to ever get stuck! Stick with it past that initial 15% mark and you're in for quite an intellectually stimulating ride.<br /><br />5. If you love Italy or the quintessential proper English culture and decor, you will enjoy this novel. The only thing that bugged me from time to time was not really knowing enough about Philip prior to meeting Rachel, so I could form a strong enough opinion on who he was as a person, i.e. before he became mesmerized by his cousin Rachel.<br /><br />6. My favorite part of the whole book... Philip ALWAYS refers to her as 'My cousin Rachel' until a certain event changes their lives... then she simply becomes 'Rachel.' The meaning of the novel is hidden in that ever-so-small alteration in their relationship and future.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <u>Summary</u>
  </i>
</b><br />du Maurier is quite skilled at creating scenery, characters, and undetermined truth. We really never know who to believe, even in the end. But it works. Whereas Rebecca was a stronger plot, I think My Cousin Rachel pushes the envelope more in terms of who should we believe. Either case, I really enjoyed the read, especially discussing it with Michael, who is an author you might want to take a look at (new book coming out in April '18).  I plan to review the author's bibliography this summer to see if there's another potential novel of hers I'd like to read. Overall, I'd give this 4+ stars as I really enjoyed it, but there was some repetition and missing pieces so I couldn't quite knock it up to a 5-star rating.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2609624943120653137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
